Preface
This is a set of exercises and problems for a (more or less) standard beginning calculus sequence.
While a fair number of the exercises involve only routine computations, many of the exercises and
most of the problems are meant to illuminate points that in my experience students have found
confusing.
Virtually all of the exercises have Ô¨Āll-in-the-blank type answers. Often an exercise will end
with something like, ‚Äú . . . so the answer is a
‚ąö
3 + ŌÄ
b where a =
and b =
.‚ÄĚ One
advantage of this type of answer is that it makes it possible to provide students with feedback on a
substantial number of homework exercises without a huge investment of time. More importantly,
it gives students a way of checking their work without giving them the answers. When a student
works through the exercise and comes up with an answer that doesn‚Äôt look anything like a
‚ąö
3 + ŌÄ
b ,
he/she has been given an obvious invitation to check his/her work.
The major drawback of this type of answer is that it does nothing to promote good communi-
cation skills, a matter which in my opinion is of great importance even in beginning courses. That
is what the problems are for. They require logically thought through, clearly organized, and clearly
written up reports. In my own classes I usually assign problems for group work outside of class.
This serves the dual purposes of reducing the burden of grading and getting students involved in
the material through discussion and collaborative work.
This collection is divided into parts and chapters roughly by topic. Many chapters begin with
a ‚Äúbackground‚ÄĚ section. This is most emphatically not intended to serve as an exposition of the
relevant material.
It is designed only to Ô¨Āx notation, deÔ¨Ānitions, and conventions (which vary
widely from text to text) and to clarify what topics one should have studied before tackling the
exercises and problems that follow.
The Ô¨āood of elementary calculus texts published in the past half century shows, if nothing else,
that the topics discussed in a beginning calculus course can be covered in virtually any order. The
divisions into chapters in these notes, the order of the chapters, and the order of items within a
chapter is in no way intended to reÔ¨āect opinions I have about the way in which (or even if) calculus
should be taught. For the convenience of those who might wish to make use of these notes I have
simply chosen what seems to me one fairly common ordering of topics. Neither the exercises nor the
problems are ordered by diÔ¨Éculty. Utterly trivial problems sit alongside ones requiring substantial
thought.
Each chapter ends with a list of the solutions to all the odd-numbered exercises.
The great majority of the ‚Äúapplications‚ÄĚ that appear here, as in most calculus texts, are best
regarded as jests whose purpose is to demonstrate in the very simplest ways some connections
between physical quantities (area of a Ô¨Āeld, volume of a silo, speed of a train, etc.)
and the
mathematics one is learning. It does not make these ‚Äúreal world‚ÄĚ problems. No one seriously
imagines that some Farmer Jones is really interested in maximizing the area of his necessarily
rectangular stream-side pasture with a Ô¨Āxed amount of fencing, or that your friend Sally just
happens to notice that the train passing her is moving at 54.6 mph.
To my mind genuinely
interesting ‚Äúreal world‚ÄĚ problems require, in general, way too much background to Ô¨Āt comfortably
into an already overstuÔ¨Äed calculus course. You will Ô¨Ānd in this collection just a very few serious
applications, problem 15 in Chapter 29, for example, where the background is either minimal or
largely irrelevant to the solution of the problem.
ix


x
PREFACE
I make no claims of originality. While I have dreamed up many of the items included here,
there are many others which are standard calculus exercises that can be traced back, in one form or
another, through generations of calculus texts, making any serious attempt at proper attribution
quite futile. If anyone feels slighted, please contact me.
There will surely be errors. I will be delighted to receive corrections, suggestions, or criticism
at
erdman@pdx.edu
I have placed the the LATEX source Ô¨Āles on my web page so that anyone who wishes can download
the material, edit it, add to it, and use it for any noncommercial purpose.

CHAPTER 1
INEQUALITIES AND ABSOLUTE VALUES
1.1. Background
Topics: inequalities, absolute values.
1.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. If x and a are two real numbers the distance between x and a is |x ‚ąía|. For
most purposes in calculus it is better to think of an inequality like |x ‚ąí5| < 2 geometrically rather
then algebraically. That is, think ‚ÄúThe number x is within 2 units of 5,‚ÄĚ rather than ‚ÄúThe absolute
value of x minus 5 is strictly less than 2.‚ÄĚ The Ô¨Ārst formulation makes it clear that x is in the open
interval (3, 7).
1.1.2. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let a be a real number.
A neighborhood of a is an open interval (c, d)
in R which contains a. An open interval (a ‚ąíőī, a + őī) which is centered at a is a symmetric
neighborhood (or a őī-neighborhood) of a.
1.1.3. DeÔ¨Ānition. A deleted (or punctured) neighborhood of a point a ‚ąąR is an open
interval around a from which a has been deleted. Thus, for example, the deleted őī-neighborhood
about 3 would be (3 ‚ąíőī, 3 + őī) \ {3} or, using diÔ¨Äerent notation, (3 ‚ąíőī, 3) ‚ą™(3, 3 + őī).
1.1.4. DeÔ¨Ānition. A point a is an accumulation point of a set B ‚äÜR if every deleted neigh-
borhood of a contains at least one point of B.
1.1.5. Notation (For Set Operations). Let A and B be subsets of a set S. Then
(1) x ‚ąąA ‚ą™B if x ‚ąąA or x ‚ąąB
(union);
(2) x ‚ąąA ‚ą©B if x ‚ąąA and x ‚ąąB
(intersection);
(3) x ‚ąąA \ B if x ‚ąąA and x /‚ąąB
(set diÔ¨Äerence); and
(4) x ‚ąąAc if x ‚ąąS \ A
(complement).
If the set S is not speciÔ¨Āed, it is usually understood to be the set R of real numbers or, starting in
Part 6, the set Rn, Euclidean n-dimensional space.

CHAPTER 2
LINES IN THE PLANE
2.1. Background
Topics: equations of lines in the plane, slope, x- and y-intercepts, parallel and perpendicular lines.
2.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let (x1, y1) and (x2, y2) be points in the plane such that x1Őł = x2. The slope
of the (nonvertical straight) line L which passes through these points is
mL := y2 ‚ąíy1
x2 ‚ąíx1
.
The equation for L is
y ‚ąíy0 = mL(x ‚ąíx0)
where (x0, y0) is any point lying on L. (If the line L is vertical (that is, parallel to the y-axis) it
is common to say that it has inÔ¨Ānite slope and write mL = ‚ąě. The equation for a vertical line is
x = x0 where (x0, y0) is any point lying on L.)
Two nonvertical lines L and L ‚Ä≤ are parallel if their respective slopes mL and mL ‚Ä≤ are equal.
(Any two vertical lines are parallel.) They are perpendicular if their respective slopes are negative
reciprocals; that is, if mL ‚Ä≤ =
1
mL
. (Vertical lines are always perpendicular to horizontal lines.)

2.3. PROBLEMS
9
2.3. Problems
(1) The town of PlainÔ¨Āeld is 4 miles east and 6 miles north of Burlington. Allentown is 8
miles west and 1 mile north of PlainÔ¨Āeld. A straight road passes through PlainÔ¨Āeld and
Burlington. A second straight road passes through Allentown and intersects the Ô¨Ārst road
at a point somewhere south and west of Burlington. The angle at which the roads intersect
is ŌÄ/4 radians. Explain how to Ô¨Ānd the location of the point of intersection and carry out
the computation you describe.
(2) Prove that the line segment joining the midpoints of two sides of a triangle is half the
length of the third side and is parallel to it. Hint. Try not to make things any more
complicated than they need to be. A thoughtful choice of a coordinate system may be
helpful. One possibility: orient the triangle so that one side runs along the x-axis and one
vertex is at the origin.

CHAPTER 3
FUNCTIONS
3.1. Background
Topics: functions, domain, codomain, range, bounded above, bounded below, composition of
functions.
3.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. If S and T are sets we say that f is a function from S to T if for every x
in S there corresponds one and only one element f(x) in T. The set S is called the domain of f
and is denoted by dom f. The set T is called the codomain of f. The range of f is the set of
all f(x) such that x belongs to S. It is denoted by ran f. The words function, map, mapping, and
transformation are synonymous.
A function f : A ‚ÜíB is said to be real valued if B ‚äÜR and is called a function of a real variable
if A ‚äÜR.
The notation f : S ‚ÜíT : x 7‚Üíf(x) indicates that f is a function whose domain is S, whose
codomain is T, and whose value at x is f(x). Thus, for example, f : R ‚ÜíR: x 7‚Üíx2 deÔ¨Ānes the
real valued function whose value at each real number x is given by f(x) = x2. We use dom f to
denote the domain of f and ran f to denote its range.
3.1.2. DeÔ¨Ānition. A function f : S ‚ÜíR is bounded above by a number M is f(x) ‚Č§M for
every x ‚ąąS, It is bounded below by a number K if K ‚Č§f(x) for every x ‚ąąS. And it is
bounded if it is bounded both above and below; that is, if there exists N > 0 such that |f(x)| ‚Č§N
for every x ‚ąąS.
3.1.3. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let f and g be real valued functions of a real variable. DeÔ¨Āne the composite
of g and f, denoted by g ‚ó¶f, by
(g ‚ó¶f)(x) := g(f(x))
for all x ‚ąądom f such that f(x) ‚ąądom g. The operation ‚ó¶is called composition.
For problem 2, the following fact may be useful.
3.1.4. Theorem. Every nonempty open interval in R contains both rational and irrational numbers.

3.3. Problems
(1) Do there exist functions f and g deÔ¨Āned on R such that
f(x) + g(y) = xy
for all real numbers x and y? Explain.
(2) Your friend Susan has become interested in functions f : R ‚ÜíR which preserve both the
operation of addition and the operation of multiplication; that is, functions f which satisfy
f(x + y) = f(x) + f(y)
(3.1)
and
f(xy) = f(x)f(y)
(3.2)
for all x, y ‚ąąR. Naturally she started her investigation by looking at some examples. The
trouble is that she was able to Ô¨Ānd only two very simple examples: f(x) = 0 for all x and
f(x) = x for all x. After expending considerable eÔ¨Äort she was unable to Ô¨Ānd additional
examples. She now conjectures that there are no other functions satisfying (3.1) and (3.2).
Write Susan a letter explaining why she is correct.
Hint. You may choose to pursue the following line of argument. Assume that f is a
function (not identically zero) which satisÔ¨Āes (3.1) and (3.2) above.
(a) Show that f(0) = 0. [In (3.1) let y = 0.]
(b) Show that if aŐł = 0 and a = ab, then b = 1.
(c) Show that f(1) = 1.
[How do we know that there exists a number c such that
f(c)Őł = 0? Let x = c and y = 1 in (3.2).]
(d) Show that f(n) = n for every natural number n.
(e) Show that f(‚ąín) = ‚ąín for every natural number n. [Let x = n and y = ‚ąín in (3.1).
Use (d).]
(f) Show that f(1/n) = 1/n for every natural number n.
[Let x = n and y = 1/n
in (3.2).]
(g) Show that f(r) = r for every rational number r. [If r ‚Č•0 write r = m/n where
m and n are natural numbers; then use (3.2), (d), and (e). Next consider the case
r < 0.]
(h) Show that if x ‚Č•0, then f(x) ‚Č•0. [Write x as ‚ąöx‚ąöx and use (3.2).]
(i) Show that if x ‚Č§y, then f(x) ‚Č§f(y). [Show that f(‚ąíx) = ‚ąíf(x) holds for all real
numbers x. Use (h).]
(j) Now prove that f must be the identity function on R.
[Argue by contradiction:
Assume f(x)Őł = x for some number x. Then there are two possibilities: either f(x) > x
or f(x) < x. Show that both of these lead to a contradiction. Apply theorem 3.1.4
to the two cases f(x) > x and f(x) < x to obtain the contradiction f(x) < f(x).]
(3) Let f(x) = 1 ‚ąíx and g(x) = 1/x. Taking composites of these two functions in all possible
ways (f ‚ó¶f, g ‚ó¶f, f ‚ó¶g ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶f, g ‚ó¶g ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶g ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶f, etc.), how many distinct functions can
be produced? Write each of the resulting functions in terms of f and g. How do you know
there are no more? Show that each function on your list has an inverse which is also on
your list. What is the common domain for these functions? That is, what is the largest
set of real numbers for which all these functions are deÔ¨Āned?
(4) Prove or disprove: composition of functions is commutative; that is g ‚ó¶f = f ‚ó¶g when
both sides are deÔ¨Āned.
(5) Let f, g, h: R ‚ÜíR. Prove or disprove: f ‚ó¶(g + h) = f ‚ó¶g + f ‚ó¶h.
(6) Let f, g, h: R ‚ÜíR. Prove or disprove: (f + g) ‚ó¶h = (f ‚ó¶h) + (g ‚ó¶h).

4.1. Background
Topics: limit of f(x) as x approaches a, limit of f(x) as x approaches inÔ¨Ānity, left- and right-hand
limits.
4.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. Suppose that f is a real valued function of a real variable, a is an accumulation
point of the domain of f, and ‚Ąď‚ąąR. We say that ‚Ąďis the limit of f(x) as x approaches a if for
every neighborhood V of ‚Ąďthere exists a corresponding deleted neighborhood U of a which satisÔ¨Āes
the following condition:
for every point x in the domain of f which lies in U the point f(x) lies in V .
Once we have convinced ourselves that in this deÔ¨Ānition it doesn‚Äôt matter if we work only with
symmetric neighborhoods of points, we can rephrase the deÔ¨Ānition in a more conventional algebraic
fashion: ‚Ąďis the limit of f(x) as x approaches a provided that for every ŌĶ > 0 there exists őī > 0
such that if 0 < |x ‚ąía| < őī and x ‚ąądom f, then |f(x) ‚ąí‚Ąď| < ŌĶ.
4.1.2. Notation. To indicate that a number ‚Ąďis the limit of f(x) as x approaches a, we may write
either
lim
x‚Üía f(x) = l
or
f(x) ‚Üí‚Ąďas x ‚Üía.
(See problem 2.)

CHAPTER 5
CONTINUITY
5.1. Background
Topics: continuous functions, intermediate value theorem. extreme value theorem.
There are many ways of stating the intermediate value theorem. The simplest says that con-
tinuous functions take intervals to intervals.
5.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. A subset J of the real line R is an interval if z ‚ąąJ whenever a, b ‚ąąJ and
a < z < b.
5.1.2. Theorem (Intermediate Value Theorem). Let J be an interval in R and f : J ‚ÜíR be
continuous. Then the range of f is an interval.
5.1.3. DeÔ¨Ānition. A real-valued function f on a set A is said to have a maximum at a point a in
A if f(a) ‚Č•f(x) for every x in A; the number f(a) is the maximum value of f. The function has
a minimum at a if f(a) ‚Č§f(x) for every x in A; and in this case f(a) is the minimum value of
f. A number is an extreme value of f if it is either a maximum or a minimum value. It is clear
that a function may fail to have maximum or minimum values. For example, on the open interval
(0, 1) the function f : x 7‚Üíx assumes neither a maximum nor a minimum.
The concepts we have just deÔ¨Āned are frequently called global (or absolute) maximum and
global (or absolute) minimum.
5.1.4. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let f : A ‚ÜíR where A ‚äÜR. The function f has a local (or relative)
maximum at a point a ‚ąąA if there exists a neighborhood J of a such that f(a) ‚Č•f(x) whenever
x ‚ąąJ and x ‚ąądom f. It has a local (or relative) minimum at a point a ‚ąąA if there exists a
neighborhood J of a such that f(a) ‚Č§f(x) whenever x ‚ąąJ and x ‚ąądom f.
5.1.5. Theorem (Extreme Value Theorem). Every continuous real valued function on a closed
and bounded interval in R achieves its (global) maximum and minimum value at some points in the
interval.
5.1.6. DeÔ¨Ānition. A number p is a fixed point of a function f : R ‚ÜíR if f(p) = p.
5.1.7. Example. If f(x) = x2 ‚ąí6 for all x ‚ąąR, then 3 is a Ô¨Āxed point of f.

CHAPTER 7
TECHNIQUES OF DIFFERENTIATION
7.1. Background
Topics: rule for diÔ¨Äerentiating products, rule for diÔ¨Äerentiating quotients, chain rule, tangent lines,
implicit diÔ¨Äerentiation.
7.1.1. Notation. We use f(n)(a) to denote the nth derivative of f at a.
7.1.2. DeÔ¨Ānition. A point a in the domain of a function f is a stationary point of f is
f ‚Ä≤(a) = 0. It is a critical point of f if it is either a stationary point of f or if it is a point where
the derivative of f does not exist.
Some authors use the terms stationary point and critical point interchangeably‚ÄĒespecially in
higher dimensions.

CHAPTER 8
THE MEAN VALUE THEOREM
8.1. Background
Topics: Rolle‚Äôs theorem, the mean value theorem, the intermediate value theorem.
8.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. A real valued function f deÔ¨Āned on an interval J is increasing on J if f(a) ‚Č§
f(b) whenever a, b ‚ąąJ and a ‚Č§b. It is strictly increasing on J if f(a) < f(b) whenever a,
b ‚ąąJ and a < b. The function f is decreasing on J if f(a) ‚Č•f(b) whenever a, b ‚ąąJ and a ‚Č§b.
It is strictly decreasing on J if f(a) > f(b) whenever a, b ‚ąąJ and a < b.
NOTE: In many texts the word ‚Äúnondecreasing‚ÄĚ is used where ‚Äúincreasing‚ÄĚ in these notes; and
‚Äúincreasing‚ÄĚ is used for ‚Äústrictly increasing‚ÄĚ.

CHAPTER 10
MONOTONICITY AND CONCAVITY
10.1. Background
Topics: increasing, decreasing, monotone, concave up, concave down.
10.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. A real valued function f deÔ¨Āned on an interval J is increasing on J if
f(a) ‚Č§f(b) whenever a, b ‚ąąJ and a ‚Č§b. It is strictly increasing on J if f(a) < f(b) whenever
a, b ‚ąąJ and a < b. The function f is decreasing on J if f(a) ‚Č•f(b) whenever a, b ‚ąąJ and
a ‚Č§b. It is strictly decreasing on J if f(a) > f(b) whenever a, b ‚ąąJ and a < b.
10.1.2. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let f : A ‚ÜíR where A ‚äÜR. The function f has a local (or relative)
maximum at a point a ‚ąąA if there exists r > 0 such that f(a) ‚Č•f(x) whenever |x ‚ąía| < r and
x ‚ąądom f. It has a local (or relative) minimum at a point a ‚ąąA if there exists r > 0 such
that f(a) ‚Č§f(x) whenever |x ‚ąía| < r and x ‚ąądom f. The point a is a relative extremum of
f if it is either a relative maximum or a relatives minimum.
The function f : A ‚ÜíR is said to attain a maximum at a if f(a) ‚Č•f(x) for all x ‚ąądom f. This
is often called a global (or absolute) maximum to help distinguish it from the local version
deÔ¨Āned above. It is clear that every global maximum is also a local maximum but not vice versa.
(Of course, similar deÔ¨Ānitions hold for global or absolute minima and global or absolute extrema.)
10.1.3. DeÔ¨Ānition. A real valued function f deÔ¨Āned on an interval J is concave up on J if the
chord line connecting any two points (a, f(a)) and (b, f(b)) on the curve (where a, b ‚ąąJ) always
lies on or above the curve. It is concave down if the chord line always lies on or below the curve.
A point on the curve where the concavity changes is a point of inflection.
When f is twice diÔ¨Äerentiable it is concave up on J if and only if f ‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(c) ‚Č•0 for all c ‚ąąJ and
is concave down on J if and only if f ‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(c) ‚Č§0 for all c ‚ąąJ.

10.3. Problems
(1) Suppose that a function f is increasing on the interval (‚ąí‚ąě, ‚ąí5) and is also increasing
on the interval (‚ąí5, ‚ąě). Is it necessarily the case that f must be increasing on the set
(‚ąí‚ąě, ‚ąí5) ‚ą™(‚ąí5, ‚ąě)? Explain.
(2) An equation of state of a substance is an equation expressing a relationship between
the pressure P, the volume V , and the temperature T of the substance.
A van der
Waals gas is a gas for which there exist positive constants a and b (depending on the
particular gas) such that the following equation of state holds:

P + a
V 2

(V ‚ąíb) = RT.
(‚ąó)
(Here R is a universal constant, not depending on the particular gas.) For each Ô¨Āxed
value of T the equation of state (‚ąó) can be used to express P as a function of V , say
P = f(V ). A critical temperature, which we denote by Tc, is a value of T for which
the corresponding function f possesses a critical point which is also a point of inÔ¨āection.
The V and P coordinates of this critical point are denoted by Vc and Pc and are called
the critical volume and the critical pressure.
Show that every van der Waals gas has a critical temperature. Compute the critical
values RTc, Vc, and Pc (in terms of the gas constants a and b). Explain how you know
that the point (Vc, Pc) is a point of inÔ¨āection.
(3) A water storage tank consists of two parts: the bottom portion is a cylinder with radius
10 feet and height 50 feet; the top portion is a sphere of radius 25 feet. (A small bottom
portion of the sphere is missing where it connects to the cylinder.) The tank is being Ô¨Ālled
from the bottom of the cylindrical portion with water Ô¨āowing in at a constant rate of 100
cubic feet per minute. Let h(t) be the height of the water in the tank at time t. Sketch a
graph of the function h from the time the Ô¨Ālling starts to the time the tank is full. Explain
carefully the reasoning behind all properties of your graph‚ÄĒpaying particular attention
to its concavity properties.
(4) For what values of k > 0 does the function f deÔ¨Āned by
f(x) = ln x
k

kx
x + 1
have local extrema? For each such k locate and classify the extrema. Explain the reasons
for your conclusions carefully.

CHAPTER 11
INVERSE FUNCTIONS
11.1. Background
Topics: inverse functions and their derivatives, logarithmic functions, the natural logarithm, ex-
ponential functions, trigonometric and inverse trigonometric functions, implicit diÔ¨Äerentiation.
The following two facts may be helpful in solving problem 1
11.1.1. Proposition. Every real number is the limit of a sequence of rational numbers. That is,
if a is a real number, then there are rational numbers x1, x2, x3, . . . such that limn‚Üí‚ąěxn = a.
11.1.2. Proposition. If g is a continuous function and x1, x2, x3, . . . are real numbers such that
limn‚Üí‚ąěxn = a, then limn‚Üí‚ąěg(xn) = g(a).

What you must prove is that

for every x > 0.
(11.1)
The crucial result that you will need to prove is that
f(ur) = r f(u)
(11.2)

you can use propositions 11.1.1 and 11.1.2 to conclude that
f(ey) = y
for every real number y.

the case r = 1/n where n is a natural number. Use these results to show that (11.2) holds
for every positive rational number. Next deal with the case r = 0. Finally verify (11.2)
for the case where r is a negative rational number. (To do this prove that f(1/v) = ‚ąíf(v)

CHAPTER 12
APPLICATIONS OF THE DERIVATIVE
12.1. Background
Topics: antiderivatives, related rates, optimization, Newton‚Äôs method.
This chapter makes no pretense of presenting interesting ‚Äúreal-world‚ÄĚ applications of the dif-
ferential calculus. Its purpose is simply to make some elementary connections between the mathe-
matical concept of derivative and various instances of rates of change of physical quantities.
Newton‚Äôs Law of Cooling: the rate of cooling of a hot body is proportional to the diÔ¨Äerence
between its temperature and that of the surrounding medium.

CHAPTER 14
THE FUNDAMENTAL THEOREM OF CALCULUS
14.1. Background
Topics: Fundamental theorem of Calculus, diÔ¨Äerentiation of indeÔ¨Ānite integrals, evaluation of
deÔ¨Ānite integrals using antiderivatives.
The next two results are versions of the most elementary form of the fundamental theorem of
calculus. (For a much more sophisticated version see theorem 46.1.1.)
14.1.1. Theorem (Fundamental Theorem Of Calculus - Version I). Let a belong to an open interval
J in the real line and f : J ‚ÜíR be a continuous function. DeÔ¨Āne F(x) =
R x
a f for all x ‚ąąJ. Then
for each x ‚ąąJ the function F is diÔ¨Äerentiable at x and DF(x) = f(x).
14.1.2. Theorem (Fundamental Theorem of Calculus - Version II). Let a and b be points in an
open interval J ‚äÜR with a < b. If f : J ‚ÜíR is continuous and g is an antiderivative of f on J,
then
Z b
a
f = g(b) ‚ąíg(a) .
The next proposition is useful in problem 5. It says that the only circumstance in which a
diÔ¨Äerentiable function F can fail to be continuously diÔ¨Äerentiable at a point a is when either the
right- or left-hand limit of F ‚Ä≤(x) fails to exist at a.
14.1.3. Proposition. Let F be a diÔ¨Äerentiable real valued function in some open interval contain-
ing the point a. If l := limx‚Üía‚ąíF ‚Ä≤(x) and r := limx‚Üía+ F ‚Ä≤(x) both exist, then
F ‚Ä≤(a) = r = l = lim
x‚Üía F ‚Ä≤(x).
Proof. Suppose that F is diÔ¨Äerentiable on the interval (a ‚ąíőī, a + őī). For x ‚ąą(a, a + őī) the
mean value theorem guarantees the existence of a point c ‚ąą(a, x) such that
F(x) ‚ąíF(a)
x ‚ąía
= F ‚Ä≤(c).
Taking the limit as x approaches a from the right we get F ‚Ä≤(a) = r. A nearly identical argument
yields F ‚Ä≤(a) = l. This shows that F is continuously diÔ¨Äerentiable at a.

14.3. PROBLEMS
103
Hints for solution. When working with the Ô¨Ārst function h it is possible to get the
‚Äúright answers‚ÄĚ to questions (i)‚ÄĒ(iii) but at the same time fail to give coherent reasons
for the assertions made. This part of the problem is meant to encourage paying attention
to the precise deÔ¨Ānitions of some of the terms. Indeed, the correct answers will vary from
text to text. Some texts, for instance, distinguish between functions that are increasing
and those that are strictly increasing. Other texts replace these terms by nondecreasing
and increasing, respectively.
Some texts deÔ¨Āne concavity only for functions which are
twice diÔ¨Äerentiable; others deÔ¨Āne it in terms of the Ô¨Ārst derivative; still others deÔ¨Āne it
geometrically.
This Ô¨Ārst part of the problem also provides an opportunity to review a few basic facts:
diÔ¨Äerentiability implies continuity; continuity can be characterized (or deÔ¨Āned) in terms
of limits; and so on.
Unraveling the properties of the second function g is rather harder. Try not to be put
oÔ¨Äby the odd looking deÔ¨Ānition of g. The crucial insight here is that by carrying out
the indicated integration it is possible to express g, at least piecewise, as a polynomial.
From a polynomial expression it is a simple matter to extract the required information.
Impatience at this stage is not a reliable friend. It is not a good idea to try to carry out
the integration before you have thought through the problem and discovered the necessity
of dividing the interval into two pieces. It may be helpful to compute the values of g at
x = 1.0, 1.1, 1.2, . . . , 1.9, 2.0. Notice that about midway in these computations something
odd happens. What is the precise point p where things change? Eventually one sees that
g too is expressible as one polynomial on [0, p] and as another polynomial on (p , 2]. Once
g has been expressed piecewise by polynomials it is possible to proceed with questions
(i)‚Äď(iii). To determine whether g is continuous at p, compute the right- and left-hand
limits of g there.
The question of the diÔ¨Äerentiability of g is subtle and deserves some serious thought.
It may be tempting to carry over the format of continuity argument to decide about the
diÔ¨Äerentiability of g at p.
Suppose we compute the right- and left-hand limits of the
derivative of g at p and Ô¨Ānd that they are not equal. Can we then conclude that g is not
diÔ¨Äerentiable at p? At Ô¨Ārst one is inclined to say no, that all we have shown is that the
derivative of g is not continuous at p, which does not address the issue of the existence
of g‚Ä≤(p). Interestingly enough, it turns out that the only way in which a diÔ¨Äerentiable
function F can fail to be continuously diÔ¨Äerentiable at a point a is for either the right-
or left-hand limit of F ‚Ä≤(x) to fail to exist at a. The crucial result, which is a bit hard
to Ô¨Ānd in beginning calculus texts, is proposition 14.1.3. Thus when we discover that a
function F is diÔ¨Äerentiable at all points other than a, and that the limits limx‚Üía‚ąíF ‚Ä≤(x)
and limx‚Üía+ F ‚Ä≤(x) both exist but fail to be equal, there is only one possible explanation:
F fails to be diÔ¨Äerentiable at a.
After Ô¨Ānding a piecewise polynomial expression for g, another diÔ¨Éculty arises in deter-
mining whether g is concave up. It is easy to see that g is concave up on the intervals (0, p)
and (p , 2). But this isn‚Äôt enough to establish the property for the entire interval (0, 2).
In fact, according to the deÔ¨Ānition of concavity given in many texts g is not concave up.
Why? Because, according to Finney and Thomas (see [2], page 237), for example, concav-
ity is deÔ¨Āned only for diÔ¨Äerentiable functions. A function is concave up on an interval only
if its derivative is increasing on the interval. So if our function g fails to be diÔ¨Äerentiable at
some point it can not be concave up. On the other hand, under any reasonable geometric
deÔ¨Ānition of concavity g certainly is concave up on (0, 2)‚ÄĒalthough it is a bit hard to
show. The solution to this dilemma is straightforward: pick a deÔ¨Ānition and stick to it.
Analysis of the last function f proceeds pretty much as for g. One new wrinkle is the
diÔ¨Éculty in determining where f is positive. The point at which f changes sign is a root


104
14. THE FUNDAMENTAL THEOREM OF CALCULUS
of a Ô¨Āfth degree polynomial. An approximation based either on the intermediate value
theorem or Newton‚Äôs method goes smoothly.
As with g, conclusions concerning the concavity of f may diÔ¨Äer depending on the deÔ¨Ā-
nitions used. This time both a geometrical deÔ¨Ānition and one based on the Ô¨Ārst derivative
lead to one conclusion while a deÔ¨Ānition based on the second derivative leads to another.
Finally, for part (c) does it make any sense to regard integration as a ‚Äúsmoothing‚ÄĚ
operation? In what way?

CHAPTER 16
APPLICATIONS OF THE INTEGRAL
16.1. Background
Topics: area under a curve, long-run value of a physical quantity, half-life of a radioactive sub-
stance, area of a region in the plane, arc length of a curve, volume of a solid using the method of
cross-sections, volume of a solid using the shell method, work, centroid of a plane region, moment
of a plane region about a line.
As with the chapter 12 on applications of the derivative there are few interesting ‚Äúreal-world‚ÄĚ
problems here. The purpose of the material is to establish a few elementary links between some
mathematical concepts and corresponding physical quantities.
Newton‚Äôs law of cooling says that the rate of cooling of a hot body is proportional to the
diÔ¨Äerence between its temperature and that of the surrounding medium.
If the position of an object is given as a twice diÔ¨Äerentiable function x of time t, the velocity
v of the object is the derivative of position; that is, v(t) = Dx(t). The acceleration a of the
object is the second derivative of position; that is a(t) = D2x(t) = Dv(t).
16.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let R be a region in the plane of area A with nonempty interior, Mx(R) be
the moment of R about the x-axis, and My(R) be the moment of R about the y-axis. Then the
centroid of R is the point (x, y) where
x = My(R)
A
and
y = Mx(R)
A
.
16.1.2. Theorem (Pappus‚Äôs Theorem). Let R be a region in the plane and L be a line which passes
through no point in the interior of R. When R is revolved once about L the volume of the resulting
solid is the product of the area of R and the distance traveled by its centroid.

16.3. Problems
The Ô¨Ārst problem below is a slight variant of the famous ‚Äúsnowplow‚ÄĚ problem, which I Ô¨Ārst
came across in Ralph Palmer Agnew‚Äôs classic text [1] on DiÔ¨Äerential Equations. This wonderful
problem is much more like a ‚Äúreal-world‚ÄĚ problem than most of the others included here in that it
requires one to make a number of rather strong assumptions (simpliÔ¨Ācations) even to get started.
(1) One morning it started snowing in Portland. A snowplow was dispatched at noon to clear
a highway. It had plowed 2 miles by 1 pm and 3 miles by 2 pm. At what time did it
begin snowing? Hint. Suppose w is the width of the plow blade, y(t) is the depth of the
snow at time t, and x(t) is the distance the plow has traveled by time t. What reasonable
assumptions might you make that would justify taking wy(t)x‚Ä≤(t) to be constant?
(2) You are considering the problem of calculating the surface area of a solid of revolution.

He says your formula is too complicated. You say his is too simple to work. Unable

author agrees with you. Fred is unimpressed; he says the author probably made the same
mistake you did. You Ô¨Ānd several more references to support your work. Fred thinks they
all probably copied from the person who made the original mistake.
Find a completely convincing argument that even skeptical Fred will accept that his
formula can‚Äôt be correct and that yours is better.
(3) The sides of a square are initially 4 inches in length and increase at a constant rate of 3
inches per second. One corner of the square moves along a line L at a speed of 2 inches

carefully how to compute the volume of the solid generated by the square. Carry out the
computation you describe.

(6) Let R be a bounded region in the plane and L a line which does not pass through any

point at which the diagonals intersect.)
(2) If R is divided into nonoverlapping subregions R1, . . . , Rn, then the moment of R
about L is the sum of the moments of the subregions Rk about L. That is,

CHAPTER 18
SEQUENCES OF REAL NUMBERS
18.1. Background
Topics: sequences, monotone (increasing and decreasing) sequences, bounded sequences, conver-
gence of sequences.
18.1.1. Notation. Technically a sequence of real numbers is a function from the set N of natural
numbers (or, occasionally, from the set Z+ of positive integers) into R. In the following material
we will denote sequences by whichever of the following four notations
a =
 an
‚ąě
n=1 = (an) = (a1, a2, a3, . . . )
seems most convenient at the moment.
In elementary algebra texts authors go to a great deal of trouble to distinguish between the
notation for Ô¨Ānite sets and for n-tuples. For example, the set containing the numbers 3 and 5
is denoted by {3, 5} while the ordered pair whose Ô¨Ārst entry is 3 and whose second entry is 5 is
denoted by (3, 5). The principal diÔ¨Äerence is that {3, 5} = {5, 3} while (3, 5)Őł = (5, 3). Sequences
have an order just as ordered pairs, ordered triples, and so on have. So it is somewhat surprising
that many authors of calculus texts choose the notation {a1, a2, a3, . . . } for sequences.
18.1.2. DeÔ¨Ānition. A sequence (an) of real numbers is increasing if an+1 ‚Č•an for every n ‚ąąN;
it is strictly increasing if an+1 > an for every n. A sequence is decreasing if an+1 ‚Č§an for
every n, and is strictly decreasing if an+1 < an for every n. A sequence is monotone if it is
either increasing or decreasing.
Here are three useful facts about convergence of sequences of real numbers.
18.1.3. Theorem (Monotone sequence theorem). Every bounded monotone sequence of real num-
bers converges.
18.1.4. Theorem. If (an) is a convergent sequence with an ‚Üíl as n ‚Üí‚ąěand f is a function
which is continuous at l, then the sequence
 f(an)

converges and
lim
n‚Üí‚ąěf(an) = f(l).

CHAPTER 19
INFINITE SERIES
19.1. Background
Topics: inÔ¨Ānite series, sum of an inÔ¨Ānite series, geometric series, Taylor series.
19.1.1. Remark. Are sequences and series the same thing?
In ordinary discourse the words
‚Äúsequence‚ÄĚ and ‚Äúseries‚ÄĚ are ordinarily used interchangeably.
There seems to be no distinction
between saying that a sequence of events led to an outcome and saying that a series of events led
to an outcome. So naturally the Ô¨Ārst thing a conscientious calculus textbook writer has to do is
make sure that students understand the diÔ¨Äerence. Sequences are ordered; they are lists; they
are functions whose domain is the set of natural numbers. Series are sums, albeit inÔ¨Ānite sums.
[Digression: it is unfortunate that in many texts the distinction is immediately muddied by using
the notation {ak} for sequences. Beginners expend much eÔ¨Äort in learning that {a, b} is a set (an
unordered pair) whereas (a, b) is an ordered pair. This leads rational students to expect {ak} to
denote a set and (ak) a sequence. It is a cruel trick to switch and use {ak} for a sequence.]
Once it is agreed that sequences are lists, what exactly are series? One approach is to think of
them as being (inÔ¨Ānite) sums of numbers. The (Ô¨Ānite) series 2+4+8 is just another way of writing
the number 14. So when we write P‚ąě
k=1 2‚ąík = 1, isn‚Äôt it clear that what we are saying is that the
inÔ¨Ānite series P‚ąě
k=1 2‚ąík is just the number 1? But this can‚Äôt be right. If an inÔ¨Ānite series is just
a number, how could we possibly make sense of the assertion that the harmonic series P‚ąě
k=1 k‚ąí1
diverges? A single number can‚Äôt diverge.
The usual way around this is to distinguish between an inÔ¨Ānite series and its sum. Given a
sequence (a1, a2, . . . ) deÔ¨Āne a new sequence (s1, s2, . . . ) by setting sn = Pn
k=1 ak for each natural
number n. Then deÔ¨Āne the inÔ¨Ānite series P‚ąě
k=1 ak to be this sequence (sn) of partial sums. So saying
that the sequence (sn) converges is the same thing as saying that the series P‚ąě
k=1 ak converges. If
sn ‚ÜíL as n ‚Üí‚ąěwe say that L is the sum of the series. Now what would be a reasonable notation
for the sum of a series? It‚Äôs hard to think of a worse choice than the one that history has conferred
on us: if a series P‚ąě
k=1 converges, we use the name of the series P‚ąě
k=1 to denote its sum. And so
P‚ąě
k=1 2‚ąík equals the number 1 while P‚ąě
k=1(‚ąí1k) doesn‚Äôt equal any number at all.
Thus it happens that every series is a sequence. It is the sequence of its partial sums. Is every
sequence a series? In other words, is every sequence the sequence of partial sums of some other
sequence? Certainly. Suppose we start with a sequence (s1, s2, . . . ). Construct a new sequence
by letting a1 = s1 and for n > 1 let an = sn ‚ąísn‚ąí1. Then it is clear that our original sequence
(s1, s2, . . . ) is the sequence of partial sums of the sequence (a1, a2, . . . ), that is to say, an inÔ¨Ānite
series. So every sequence is an inÔ¨Ānite series. This leads to a nice puzzle. Since every sequence is
a series and every series is a sequence, can the two concepts really be distinguished?

= S .

CHAPTER 21
POWER SERIES
21.1. Background
Topics: power series, Maclaurin series, Taylor series, Lagrange form of the remainder, binomial
series, radius of convergence, interval of convergence, use of power series to solve diÔ¨Äerential equa-
tions.
Despite the impression given my many beginning calculus texts the natural habitat of power
series is the Ô¨Āeld of complex numbers‚ÄĒnot the real number line. Here is an example to illustrate
this point.
Consider two functions: the sine and the arctangent. They are both bounded and inÔ¨Ānitely
diÔ¨Äerentiable. The arctangent is extraordinarily well behaved: it is strictly increasing and changes
concavity only once. The sine function, on the other hand, not only changes from increasing to
decreasing inÔ¨Ānitely often, but also changes concavity inÔ¨Ānitely often. Why then should it turn
out that the sine function has an inÔ¨Ānite radius of convergence while the apparently much nicer
arctangent function has radius of convergence of only one? When we think of these functions as
being deÔ¨Āned only on the real line this behavior seems totally inexplicable. But if we regard them
as functions on the complex plane, everything seems quite natural. The arctangent function and its
derivative, the function z 7‚Üí
 1 + z2‚ąí1 must have the same radius of convergence, and the latter
function clearly blows up at z = i.
One calculus text that discusses complex power series is Spivak‚Äôs beautiful classic [4].

Part 6
SCALAR FIELDS AND VECTOR FIELDS


CHAPTER 22
VECTOR AND METRIC PROPERTIES of Rn
22.1. Background
Topics:
Rn, vectors in Rn, addition of vectors, scalars, scalar multiplication, inner product,
Schwarz inequality, perpendicularity (orthogonality), norm of a vector, unit vector, cross prod-
ucts, neighborhood of a point, deleted neighborhood, distance, open set, closed set, scalar Ô¨Āelds,
vector Ô¨Āelds, standard basis vectors, equations of a line in R3, equation of a plane in R3.
22.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. The set Rn of all n-tuples of real numbers is Euclidean n-dimensional space‚ÄĒ
or more brieÔ¨āy, just n-space. We make a standard notational convention. If x belongs to Rn, then
x is the n-tuple whose coordinates are x1, x2, . . . , xn; that is,
x = (x1, x2, . . . , xn) .
It must be confessed that we do not always use this convention. For example, the temptation to
denote a member of R3 by (x, y, z), rather than by (x1, x2, x3), is usually just too strong to resist.
We will often refer to the elements of Rn as vectors and real numbers as scalars. We give Rn
the structure of a vector space by deÔ¨Āning operations of addition and scalar multiplication. For
n-tuples x = (x1, x2, . . . , xn) and y = (y1, y2, . . . , yn) in Rn deÔ¨Āne
x + y := (x1 + y1, x2 + y2, . . . , xn + yn) .
Thus we say that addition in Rn is deÔ¨Āned coordinatewise. Scalar multiplication is also deÔ¨Āned in
a coordinatewise fashion. That is, if x = (x1, x2, . . . , xn) ‚ąąRn and őĪ ‚ąąR, then we deÔ¨Āne
őĪx := (őĪx1, őĪx2, . . . , őĪxn) .
It is convenient to deÔ¨Āne the distance between two points in Rn in terms of a scalar valued
function deÔ¨Āned on pairs of vectors called an inner product. If x = (x1, . . . , xn) and y = (y1, . . . , yn)
are vectors in Rn, then the inner product (or dot product) of x and y, denoted by ‚ü®x, y‚ü©, is

The distance between two vectors in Rn is the length of their diÔ¨Äerence. If x and y are vectors
in Rn deÔ¨Āne

This is the distance between x and y. (Here, as in real arithmetic, x ‚ąíy means x + (‚ąí1)y.)
We generalize the notion of őī-neighborhood (see deÔ¨Ānition 1.1.2) in R to open balls of radius őī
in Rn. Let a be a vector in Rn and őī > 0. The open ball about a of radius őī is deÔ¨Āned by
Bőī(a) := {x ‚ąąRn : d(x, a) < őī}.
We will also refer to this set as the
őī-neighborhood of a. The deleted őī-neighborhood of the point a ‚ąąRn is the open ball
around a of radius őī from which the point a has been deleted. More generally, we will refer to any
open set containing the point a as a neighborhood of a.
A subset of Rn is open if it is a union of open balls in Rn. A set is closed if its complement
is open.
22.1.3. DeÔ¨Ānition. If x = (x1, x2, x3) and y = (y1, y2, y3) are vectors in R3, then their cross
product, denoted by x √ó y, is the vector (x2y3 ‚ąíx3y2, x3y1 ‚ąíx1y3, x1y2 ‚ąíx2y1).
22.1.4. Notation. DeÔ¨Āne vectors e1, . . . , en in Rn by

(Note that the superscripts here have nothing to do with powers.) In R3 the three standard basis
vectors are often denoted by i, j, and k rather than e1, e2, and e3, respectively.

22.1.5. DeÔ¨Ānition. In these exercise/problem sets we restrict our attention to functions F which
map one Euclidean space, say Rn, to another, say Rm. In the Ô¨Ārst twenty chapters the emphasis has
been on the case where n = m = 1, real valued functions of a real variable. Curves are mappings
f : R ‚ÜíRm (where m ‚Č•2). Thus curves are vector valued functions of a real variable. Much of
the subsequent material in calculus is devoted to the study of scalar Ô¨Āelds. These are real valued
(that is, scalar valued) functions of a vector variable; that is, mappings f : Rn ‚ÜíR (where n ‚Č•2).
As we will see shortly, scalar Ô¨Āelds are also called 0-forms.
Also of importance are vector Ô¨Āelds These are mappings of Rn into Rm (where n, m ‚Č•2).
Thus a vector Ô¨Āeld is a vector valued function of a vector variable. Such a mapping F: Rn ‚ÜíRm
comprises m coordinate functions F = (F 1, F 2, . . . , F m), where each F k is itself a scalar Ô¨Āeld. Thus
for each vector x = (x1, x2, . . . , xn) in the domain of F we have
F(x) =
 F 1(x), F 2(x), . . . , F m(x)

.

23.1. Background
Topics: scalar Ô¨Āelds, limits of scalar Ô¨Āelds.
An important, if rather obvious, theorem says that a scalar Ô¨Āeld has zero limit at a point in Rn
if and only if its absolute value does.
23.1.1. Theorem. Let f be a scalar Ô¨Āeld deÔ¨Āned in a neighborhood of a point a in Rn but not
necessarily at a. Then
lim
x
/ a f(x) = 0
if and only if
lim
x
/ a |f(x)| = 0 .
The next theorem, also very useful, is sometimes referred to as the sandwich theorem for scalar
Ô¨Āelds.
23.1.2. Theorem. Let f, g, and h be scalar Ô¨Āelds deÔ¨Āned in a deleted neighborhood U of a point
a in Rn.
If f(x) ‚Č§g(x) ‚Č§h(x) for all x ‚ąąU,

25.3. Problems
(1) Let ŌÜ(x, y) = 2x2 + 6y2 and a = (2, ‚ąí1). Find the steepest downhill path on the surface
z = ŌÜ(x, y) starting at the point a and ending at the minimum point on the surface.
Hints.
(a) It is enough to Ô¨Ānd the equation of the projection of the curve onto the xy-plane;
every curve t 7‚Üí(x(t), y(t)) in the xy-plane is the projection along the z-axis of a
unique curve t 7‚Üí
 x(t), y(t), ŌÜ(x(t), y(t))

on the surface z = ŌÜ(x, y).
(b) If c: t 7‚Üí(x(t), y(t)) is the desired curve and we set c(0) = a, then the unit vector
u which minimizes the directional derivative Du(b) at a point b in R2 is the one
obtained by choosing u to point in the direction of ‚ąí‚ąáŌÜ(b). Thus in order for the
curve to point in the direction of the most rapid decrease of ŌÜ at each point c(t), the
tangent vector to the curve at c(t) must be some positive multiple p(t) of ‚ąí‚ąáŌÜ(c(t)).
The function p will govern the speed of descent; since this is irrelevant in the present
problem, set p(t) = 1 for all t.
(c) Recall that on an interval the only nonzero solution to an equation of the form Dx(t) =
kx(t) has the form x(t) = x(0) ekt.
(d) The parameter t which we have introduced is artiÔ¨Ācial. Eliminate it to obtain an
equation of the form y = f(x).
(2) This, like the preceding problem, is a steepest descent problem. However, we now suppose
(probably contrary to fact) that for some reason we are unable to solve explicitly the
resulting diÔ¨Äerential equations. Instead we invoke an approximation technique. Let
ŌÜ(x) = 13x12 ‚ąí42x1 + 13x22 + 6x2 + 10x1x2 + 9
for all x in R2. The goal is to approximate the path of steepest descent. Start at an
arbitrary point x0 in R2 and choose a number h > 0. At x0 compute the gradient of ŌÜ,
take u0 to be the unit vector pointing in the direction of ‚ąí‚ąáŌÜ(x0), and then move h units
in the direction of u0 arriving at a point x1. Repeat the procedure: Ô¨Ānd the unit vector u1
in the direction of ‚ąí‚ąáŌÜ(x1), then from x1 move h units along u1 to a point x2. Continue
in this fashion. In other words, x0 ‚ąąR2 and h > 0 are arbitrary, and for n ‚Č•0
xn+1 = xn + hun
where un = ‚ąí‚ą•‚ąáŌÜ(xn)‚ą•‚ąí1‚ąáŌÜ(xn) .

attempt to do this by hand. Write a program for a computer or a programmable
calculator. In writing your program don‚Äôt ignore the possibility that ‚ąáŌÜ(xn) may be
zero for some n. Also don‚Äôt forget when you write up your report that the reader
probably has no idea how to read the language in which you write your program. It
must be well enough documented so that the reader can easily understand what you
are doing at each step.
(b) Describe what happens when h is ‚Äútoo small‚ÄĚ. Again start at the origin, take h =
0.001 and compute 25 or 30 values of xn.
(c) By altering the values of h at appropriate times, Ô¨Ānd a succession of points x0, . . . , xn
(starting with x0 = (0, 0) ) such that the distance between xn and the point where ŌÜ
assumes its minimum value is less than 0.001. (By examining the points x0, . . . , xn

26. MATRICES AND DETERMINANTS
Some Facts about Determinants. Determinants are a useful tool for dealing with matrices
(and the linear transformations they represent).
Fact 1. Let n ‚ąąN and Mn√ón be the collection of all n √ó n matrices. There is exactly one function
det: Mn√ón
/ R: A 7‚Üídet A
which satisÔ¨Āes
(a) det In = 1.
(b) If A ‚ąąMn√ón and A‚Ä≤ is the matrix obtained by interchanging two rows of A, then det A‚Ä≤ =
‚ąídet A.
(c) If A ‚ąąMn√ón, c ‚ąąR, and A‚Ä≤ is the matrix obtained by multiplying each element in one
row of A by the number c, then det A‚Ä≤ = c det A.
(d) If A ‚ąąMn√ón, c ‚ąąR, and A‚Ä≤ is the matrix obtained from A by multiplying one row of A
by c and adding it to another row of A (that is, choose i and j between 1 and n with iŐł = j
and replace Aj
k by Aj
k + cAi
k for 1 ‚Č§k ‚Č§n), then det A‚Ä≤ = det A.
26.1.4. DeÔ¨Ānition. The unique function det: Mn√ón
/ R described above is the n √ó n deter-
minant function.
Fact 2. If A ‚ąąR ( = M1√ó1), then det A = A; if A ‚ąąM2√ó2, then det A = A1
1A2
2 ‚ąíA1
2A2
1.
Fact 3. If A, B ‚ąąMn√ón, then det(AB) = (det A)(det B).
Fact 4. If A ‚ąąMn√ón, then det At = det A. (An obvious corollary of this: in conditions (b), (c),
and (d) of fact 1 the word ‚Äúcolumns‚ÄĚ may be substituted for the word ‚Äúrows‚ÄĚ.)
26.1.5. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let A be an n √ó n matrix. The minor of the element Aj
k, denoted by Mj
k, is
the determinant of the (n ‚ąí1) √ó (n ‚ąí1) matrix which results from the deletion of the jth row and
kth column of A. The cofactor of the element Aj
k, denoted by Cj
k is deÔ¨Āned by
Cj
k := (‚ąí1)j+kMj
k.
Fact 5. If A ‚ąąMn√ón and 1 ‚Č§j ‚Č§n, then

This is the (Laplace) expansion of the determinant along the jth row.

CHAPTER 27
LINEAR MAPS
27.1. Background
Topics: vector spaces, linear maps (transformations),
27.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let V be a set.
Suppose there is an operation (called addition) which
associates with each pair x and y of elements of V an element x + y of V . And suppose there is a
second operation (called scalar multiplication) which associates with each real number őĪ and
each member x of V an element őĪ ¬∑ x (or just őĪx) in V . Then V is said to be a vector space if
the following conditions are satisÔ¨Āed:
(I) Addition is associative. That is,
x + (y + z) = (x + y) + z
for all x, y, z ‚ąąV .
(II) In V there is an element 0 (called the zero vector) such that
x + 0 = x
for all x ‚ąąV .
(III) For each x in V there is a corresponding element ‚ąíx (the additive inverse of x) such
that
x + (‚ąíx) = 0.
(IV) Addition is commutative. That is,
x + y = y + x
for all x, y ‚ąąV .
(V) If őĪ ‚ąąR and x, y ‚ąąV , then
őĪ(x + y) = (őĪx) + (őĪy).
(VI) If őĪ, ő≤ ‚ąąR and x ‚ąąV , then
(őĪ + ő≤)x = (őĪx) + (ő≤x).
(VII) If őĪ, ő≤ ‚ąąR and x ‚ąąV , then
őĪ(ő≤x) = (őĪő≤)x.
(VIII) If x ‚ąąV , then
1 ¬∑ x = x.
An element of V is a vector; an element of R is, in this context, often called a scalar. Concern-
ing the order of performing operations, we agree that scalar multiplication takes precedence over
addition. Thus, for example, condition (V) above may be unambiguously written as
őĪ(x + y) = őĪx + őĪy.
(Notice that the parentheses on the left may not be omitted.) If x and y are vectors, we deÔ¨Āne
x ‚ąíy to be x + (‚ąíy).

216
27. LINEAR MAPS
27.1.2. DeÔ¨Ānition. A function T : V
/ W between vector spaces is linear if
T(x + y) = T(x) + T(y)
for all x, y ‚ąąV and
T(őĪx) = őĪT(x)
for all x ‚ąąV and őĪ ‚ąąR. A linear function is often called a linear transformation, a linear
map, or a linear operator. There are two notational oddities associated with linear maps:
(i) The value of a linear map T at a vector x in V , is usually written Tx rather than T(x).
Of course, sometimes parentheses are necessary for clarity as in the expression T(x + y).
(ii) The composite of two linear maps S : U
/V and T : V
/W is usually written TS rather
than T ‚ó¶S.
27.1.3. DeÔ¨Ānition. If T : Rn
/ Rm is a linear map, we deÔ¨Āne [T] to be the m √ó n matrix whose
entry in the jth row and kth column is (Tek)j, the jth component of the vector Tek in Rm. That
is, [T]j
k = (Tek)j. The matrix [T] is the matrix representation of T
27.1.4. Example. Let T : R4
/R3 : (w, x, y, z) 7‚Üí(w +2x+3y, 5w +6x+7y +8z, ‚ąí2x‚ąí3y ‚ąí4z).
Then T is linear and
Te1 = T(1, 0, 0, 0) = (1, 5, 0)
Te2 = T(0, 1, 0, 0) = (2, 6, ‚ąí2)
Te3 = T(0, 0, 1, 0) = (3, 7, ‚ąí3)
Te4 = T(0, 0, 0, 1) = (0, 8, ‚ąí4).
Having computed Te1, . . . , Te4, we use these as the successive columns of [T]. Thus
T =

27.1.6. Theorem. Let T, U : Rn
/ Rm be linear maps and őĪ ‚ąąR. Then
(a) [T + U] = [T] + [U], and
(b) [őĪT] = őĪ[T].
If, in addition, S : Rp
/ Rn, then [TS] = [T][S].
27.1.7. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let T : V
/ W be a linear map between vector spaces. The kernel of T,
denoted by ker T, is the set of all x ‚ąąV such that Tx = 0.

27.3. Problems
(1) Consider an interval [a, b] in R. Let C([a, b]) be the family of all continuous real valued
functions deÔ¨Āned on [a, b]. For functions f, g ‚ąąC([a, b]) deÔ¨Āne the function f + g by
(f + g)(x) := f(x) + g(x)
for all x ‚ąą[a, b].
(It should be clear that the two ‚Äú+‚ÄĚ signs in the preceding equation denote operations in
diÔ¨Äerent spaces. The one on the left (which is being deÔ¨Āned) represents addition in the
space C([a, b]); the one on the right is ordinary addition in R.) Because we specify the
value of f + g at each point x by adding the values of f and g at that point, we say that
we add f and g pointwise.
We also deÔ¨Āne scalar multiplication to be a pointwise operation. That is, if f ‚ąąC([a, b])
and őĪ ‚ąąR, then we deÔ¨Āne the function őĪf by
(őĪf)(x) := őĪ(f(x))
for every x ‚ąą[a, b].
We know from Ô¨Ārst term calculus that according to the deÔ¨Ānitions above, both f +g and őĪf
belong to C([a, b]). (Sums of continuous functions are continuous, and constant multiples
of continuous functions are continuous.)
Prove that under these pointwise operations C([a, b]) is a vector space.
(2) Show that a vector space has at most one zero vector. That is, if 0 and 0‚Ä≤ are members
of a vector space V which satisfy x + 0 = x and x + 0‚Ä≤ = x for all x ‚ąąV , then 0 = 0‚Ä≤.
(3) Show that for every vector x in a vector space V there exists only one vector ‚ąíx such that
x + (‚ąíx) = 0 .
(4) Show that if x is a vector (in some vector space) and x + x = x, then x = 0. Hint. Add
0 to x; then write 0 as x + (‚ąíx).
(5) Let x be a vector in a vector space V and let őĪ be a real number. Prove that őĪx = 0 if
and only if x = 0 or őĪ = 0.
Hint. You need to show three things:
(a) őĪ0 = 0,
(b) 0 x = 0, and
(c) If őĪŐł = 0 and őĪx = 0, then x = 0.
To prove (a) write 0 + 0 = 0, multiply both sides by őĪ, and use a previous problem. For
(c) use the fact that if őĪ ‚ąąR is not zero, it has a reciprocal. What happens if we multiply
the vector őĪx by the scalar 1/őĪ?
(6) Suppose that S : U
/ V and T : V
/ W are linear maps between vector spaces. Show
that their composite (usually denoted by TS rather than T ‚ó¶S) is also linear.
(7) Prove that the map T : R3
/ R2 deÔ¨Āned by
T(x, y, z) = (x + y ‚ąíz, x ‚ąí2y + 3z)
is linear.
(8) Let A be a 2 √ó 2 matrix. Show that the action of A on vectors in R2 is linear. Conversely,
show that every linear map from R2 to R2 can be represented by the action of a 2 √ó 2
matrix.
Note: A more general statement is also true. (You need not prove this.) Every m √ó n
matrix acts as a linear map from Rn to Rm; and every linear map from Rn to Rm can be
represented by an m √ó n matrix.
(9) If T : V
/ W is a linear transformation between vector spaces, then it takes the zero
vector of V to the zero vector of W.

CHAPTER 28
DEFINITION OF DERIVATIVE
28.1. Background
Topics: diÔ¨Äerential = derivative = total derivative, diÔ¨Äerentiable at a point, diÔ¨Äerentiable function,
tangent plane to a surface, Jacobian matrix, smooth function.
28.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let F: Rn
/ Rm be a vector Ô¨Āeld and p be a point in Rn. DeÔ¨Āne a function
‚ąÜFp : Rn
/ Rm by
‚ąÜFp(h) = F(p + h) ‚ąíF(p)
for every h ‚ąąRn.
28.1.2. DeÔ¨Ānition. A function F: Rn
/Rm is differentiable at a point p ‚ąąRn if there exists
a linear transformation (equivalently, an m √ó n matrix) dFp taking Rn to Rm such that
lim
h
/ 0
‚ąÜFp(h) ‚ąídFp (h)
‚ą•h‚ą•
= 0 .
When this happens we say that the linear transformation (or matrix) dFp is tangent to the
function ‚ąÜFp. We call dFp the differential (or derivative, or total derivative) of F at p.
A function F is differentiable if it is diÔ¨Äerentiable at each point in its domain.

28.1.4. DeÔ¨Ānition. The matrix in the preceding theorem is the Jacobian matrix of F at p.
28.1.5. DeÔ¨Ānition. A function F: Rn
/ Rm is twice differentiable at a point p ‚ąąRn if it
is diÔ¨Äerentiable there and the function dF is also diÔ¨Äerentiable at p. Higher order diÔ¨Äerentiability
is deÔ¨Āned similarly.
28.1.6. DeÔ¨Ānition. A function F: U ‚ÜíRn, where U is an open subset of Rm, is smooth (or
infinitely differentiable) if it has derivatives of all orders.
28.1.7. Convention. When we say that a function f is diÔ¨Äerentiable (or smooth) on some region
in Rn, we will mean that it is diÔ¨Äerentiable (or smooth) on some open set in Rn which contains the
region.

CHAPTER 29
DIFFERENTIATION OF FUNCTIONS OF SEVERAL
VARIABLLES
29.1. Background
Topics: chain rule
DiÔ¨Äerentiation is about approximating (a suitable translation of) a smooth function by a linear
one. What the chain rule says (although it may be diÔ¨Écult to see this from what you are told
in many texts) is that the best linear approximation to the composite of two smooth functions is
the composite of their best linear approximations. That is, the diÔ¨Äerential of the composite is the
composite of the diÔ¨Äerentials. Here is the formal statement.
29.1.1. Theorem (The Chain Rule). If F : Rm
/Rn is diÔ¨Äerentiable at a ‚ąąRm and G: Rn
/Rp
is diÔ¨Äerentiable at F(a) ‚ąąRn, then G ‚ó¶F is diÔ¨Äerentiable at a and
d(G ‚ó¶F)a = (dG)F(a) dFa.
For computational purposes it is convenient to rephrase this theorem in terms of partial deriva-
tives.
29.1.2. Theorem (The Chain Rule). If F : Rm
/Rn is diÔ¨Äerentiable at a ‚ąąRm and G: Rn
/Rp
is diÔ¨Äerentiable at F(a) ‚ąąRn, then G ‚ó¶F is diÔ¨Äerentiable at a and
(G ‚ó¶F)k
i (a) =
n
X
j=1
Gk
j (F(a))F j
i (a)
for i = 1, . . . , m and k = 1, . . . , p.
29.1.3. Notation. Scientists like to work with variables. And frequently they use the name of a
function for its corresponding dependent variable. As a consequence of this highly dubious notation
one variable may depend explicitly and/or implicitly on another variable. For example, suppose
that w = w(x, y, t) where x = x(s, t) and y = y(s, t) and that all the functions mentioned are
diÔ¨Äerentiable. In this case we say that w depends explicitly on the variables x and y; it depends
implicitly on s; and it depends both explicitly and implicitly on t. This can lead to notational
confusion. For example, it is perhaps tempting to write

t appears explicitly in the formula for w; the one on the left takes into account the fact that in
addition w depends implicitly on t via the variables x and y.

29.1.5. DeÔ¨Ānition. A smooth function F mapping from a subset of Rn into Rn is said to be

F‚ąí1 : F(U)

CHAPTER 30
MORE APPLICATIONS OF THE DERIVATIVE
30.1. Background
Topics: optimization of functions of several variables, global extrema, local extrema, saddle points,
Lagrange multipliers.
ClassiÔ¨Ācation of Critical Points. The second derivative test as stated in many texts works
only for functions of two (or three) variables. Here is a procedure for classifying critical points of
functions of any (Ô¨Ānite) number of variables.
30.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. Let f : Rn ‚ÜíR be a smooth scalar Ô¨Āeld and p ‚ąąRn. The Hessian matrix
(or second derivative matrix) of f at p, denoted by Hf(p), is the n √ó n matrix
Hf(p) =

‚ąā2f
‚ąāxi‚ąāxj
(p)
n
i=1
n
j=1
=

fij(p)

.
30.1.2. DeÔ¨Ānition. An n √ó n matrix M is positive definite if ‚ü®Mx, x‚ü©> 0 for every xŐł = 0
in Rn. It is negative definite if ‚ü®Mx, x‚ü©< 0 for every xŐł = 0 in Rn. It is indefinite if there are
vectors x and y in Rn such that ‚ü®Mx, x‚ü©> 0 and ‚ü®My, y‚ü©< 0.
30.1.3. Theorem (Second Derivative Test). Let p be a critical point of a smooth scalar Ô¨Āeld f. If
the Hessian matrix Hf is positive deÔ¨Ānite at p, then f has a local minimum there. If Hf is negative
deÔ¨Ānite at p, then f has a local maximum there. If Hf is indeÔ¨Ānite at p, then f has a saddle point
there.
Is there some simple way of telling whether a matrix is positive deÔ¨Ānite?
Yes. It is positive deÔ¨Ānite if all its eigenvalues are strictly positive.
It is negative deÔ¨Ānite if all its eigenvalues are strictly negative. It
is indeÔ¨Ānite if it has at least one strictly positive and at least one
strictly negative eigenvalue.
What‚Äôs an eigenvalue?
An eigenvalue of a square matrix M is a root of its characteristic
polynomial.
OK. What‚Äôs a characteristic polynomial then?
Notice that if M is an n√ón matrix, then det(M‚ąíőĽIn) is a polynomial
in őĽ of degree n. This is the characteristic polynomial of M.
Incidentally, it turns out that if the matrix is symmetric (the Hessian
matrix of a smooth scalar Ô¨Āeld, for example, is symmetric), then all
the roots of the characteristic polynomial are real.
239


240
30. MORE APPLICATIONS OF THE DERIVATIVE
Summary: To classify the critical points of a scalar Ô¨Āeld f : Rn
/ R
(A) Compute ‚ąáf.
(B) Solve ‚ąáf = 0 to Ô¨Ānd the critical points.
(C) Compute the Hessian matrix Hf.
(D) For each critical point p Ô¨Ānd the roots of the polynomial det(Hf ‚ąíőĽIn).
(E) If all these roots are strictly positive, f has a local minimum at p. If they are all strictly
negative, f has a local maximum at p. And if there is at least one strictly positive and
one strictly negative root, then f has a saddle point at p.
Some deÔ¨Ānitions relevant to Problem 1.
30.1.4. DeÔ¨Ānition. A ray from the origin in R2 is a parametrized constant speed curve r: [0, ‚ąě)
/R2
such that r(0) = (0, 0) and the range of r lies in some straight line.
30.1.5. DeÔ¨Ānition. A function f : R2 ‚ÜíR is locally increasing at the origin on a ray r if the
real valued function f ‚ó¶r is increasing on some interval of the form [0, t0] (t0 > 0). The term locally
decreasing is deÔ¨Āned similarly.
30.1.6. DeÔ¨Ānition. Two collections of distinct rays (r1, . . . rn) and (R1, . . . Rn) are separating
if it is not possible to move between any two members of the Ô¨Ārst collection (in either the clockwise
or counterclockwise direction) without encountering a member of the second.

30.3. Problems
(1) Following are three plausible conjectures. In each case if the conjecture is true, prove it;
and if it is false, give an example to show that it can fail. (In the latter case, don‚Äôt forget
to prove that your example really does what you claim.)
Conjecture 1. If on every parametrized ray from the origin a function f of two variables
is locally increasing at the origin, then f has a local minimum at the origin.
Conjecture 2. If a function f of two variables has a saddle point at the origin, then there
are at least two separating pairs of rays from the origin such that f is locally increasing
at the origin on one pair and locally decreasing at the origin on the other.
Conjecture 3.
It is possible to Ô¨Ānd a function f of two variables for which there exist
two separating triples of rays from the origin such that on one of the triples f is locally
increasing at the origin and on the other f is locally decreasing at the origin.

31.3. Problems
(1) Show that the equations
Ô£Ī
Ô£ī
Ô£ī
Ô£≤
Ô£ī
Ô£ī
Ô£≥
x = 1 ‚ąít2
1 + t2
y =
2t
1 + t2
parametrize the entire unit circle except for the point (‚ąí1, 0). (It is quite easy to show
that for any number t these equations produce a point lying on the unit circle. What is
not so easy is to show that if (a, b) is a point on the unit circle other than (‚ąí1, 0), then
there exists a number t such that x(t) = a and y(t) = b.)
(2) Show that if an object moves with constant velocity, then its angular momentum (with
respect to the origin) is constant. (The angular momentum L about the origin is deÔ¨Āned
by L(t) = r(t) √ó mv(t), where m is the mass of the object, r is its position, and v its
velocity.)
(3) Explain why the curve r(t) = (2 cos 2t) i + (3 cos t) j for 0 ‚Č§t ‚Č§4ŌÄ is not a smooth curve.
Include a sketch of the curve, and on your sketch identify the points at which the tangent
vector vanishes.
(4) Fred is riding a ferris wheel at an amusement park. The radius of the wheel is 20 feet. His
friends Sally and Leslie are on the ground. Leslie sees that Sally is standing in the plane
of the ferris wheel 75 feet to the right of the bottom of the wheel. Leslie also notices that
the wheel is making 5 counterclockwise revolutions each minute. Sally throws a baseball
to Fred as he moves upwards. At the instant Fred is halfway up Sally throws the ball with
a speed of 60 ft/sec at an angle of 60‚ó¶above the horizontal. The point at which the ball
leaves Sally‚Äôs hand is the same distance from the ground as the bottom of the wheel. How
close to Fred does the ball get? Give your answer to the nearest inch.
(5) Let f be a diÔ¨Äerentiable curve. Show that ‚ą•f(t)‚ą•is constant if and only if f(t) ‚ä•Df(t)
for all t.
(6) The position of a particle at time t is given by r(t) = 1 ‚ąí2t2
1 + 4t2 i +
4t
1 + 4t2 j + 2t
‚ąö
3t2 ‚ąí1
1 + 4t2
k
for all t > 1. Without actually calculating the velocity vector v(t) explain how you know
that it must be perpendicular to the position vector r(t) for every t > 1.

(4) A parametrized curve r in R3 is a unit speed curve if its speed ‚ą•v(t)‚ą•is 1 for each t.
Recall that the length of arc between the points r(0) and r(t) on such a curve is s =
R t
0‚ą•v(ŌĄ)‚ą•dŌĄ = t; thus we may say that a unit speed curve has been parametrized by
arclength.
In this problem we wish to attach a moving orthonormal frame of coordinates to a curve
in such a way that one of the axes always points in the direction of the unit tangent vector
T and a second axis points in the direction of the unit normal N. (The third axis is called
B, for unit binormal.) When a geometric curve (as opposed to a parametrized one) has
been parametrized by arclength (or when a parametrized curve has been reparametrized
by arclength), the business of deriving properties of the moving T ‚ąíN ‚ąíB frame is fairly
straightforward. That is the task here. The idea is to write up a coherent introduction
to moving frames. Include the material in items (a)‚Äď(i) below, but do not break up your
exposition into chunks called (a), (b), (c), etc.

contains the point P and which is coplanar with the z-axis. This circle can be speciÔ¨Āed by
a single parameter őł: the angle between the positive x-axis and the vector from the origin
to the center of C. The location of P on C can also be speciÔ¨Āed by a single parameter ŌÜ:
the angle between the vector in the xy-plane from the center of C to the outer portion of
the surface T and the vector from the center of C to the point P.

35. SURFACE AREA
35.3. Problems
(1) You are considering the problem of calculating the surface area of a solid of revolution.
The curve y = f(x) (for a ‚Č§x ‚Č§b) has been rotated about the x-axis. You set up a
partition (x0, x1, . . . , xn) of [a, b] and approximate the surface area of the resulting solid by
connecting consecutive points
 xk‚ąí1, f(xk‚ąí1)

and
 xk, f(xk)

by a straight line segment,
which, when rotated about the x-axis, produces a portion of a cone. Taking the limit of
these conical approximations you end up with the integral
2ŌÄ
Z b
a
f(x)
q
1 +
 f‚Ä≤(x)
2 dx
which you claim represents the surface area of the solid.
Your friend Fred has chosen to make cylindrical approximations rather than conical
ones and produces the integral
2ŌÄ
Z b
a
f(x) dx
which he claims represents the surface area. So you and Fred argue.
He says your formula is too complicated to compute. You say his is too simple to
work. Unable to convince him, you look up the formula in a well known calculus text and
Ô¨Ānd that the author agrees with you. Fred is unimpressed; he says the author probably
made the same mistake you did. You Ô¨Ānd several more references to support your work.
Fred thinks they all probably copied from the person who made the original mistake.
Find a completely convincing argument that even skeptical Fred will accept that his
formula can‚Äôt be correct and that yours is better.
(2) In many calculus texts the authors derive the formula for the arc length of a smooth
curve by taking the limit of sums of lengths of polygonal paths inscribed in the curve.
The purpose of this problem is to inquire into the possibility of doing the ‚Äúsame thing‚ÄĚ
to Ô¨Ānd the surface area of a smooth surface.
In particular, consider a (vertical) right
circular cylinder with height h and base radius r. Partition the surface of this cylinder by
m + 1 equally spaced horizontal circles, thus creating m sub-cylinders of height h/m (and
radius r). Then partition each circle into n circular arcs by means of n equally spaced
points. (The points on one circle need not necessarily be directly above those on the circle
below.) Now approximate the surface area of each sub-cylinder by the area of triangles
inscribed in the sub-cylinder using these points as vertices. In each sub-cylinder there will
be 2n such triangles‚ÄĒn having two vertices on the upper circle and n having two vertices
on the lower circle. Thus, altogether, there will be 2mn triangles the sum of whose areas
approximates the surface area of the original cylinder.
Now, of course, the question is: what can we say about the limit of the sum of the
areas of these triangles as m and n get large? Does it give us the correct expression for
the area of a cylinder? (How, incidentally, can we be sure what the surface area of a
right circular cylinder really ought to be?) Thinking about the problem in more precise
terms, we need to ask whether this double limit even exists. If not, can we make the
corresponding iterated limits exist by restricting m and n in some way? Does the answer
depend on the relative rates of growth of m and n? What happens if we require m and n
to be in some Ô¨Āxed ratio? That is, will the limit depend on the shape of the triangles?
After considering these matters, what do you think of the prospects of generalizing
the derivation of the formula for arc length to one for surface area?

CHAPTER 39
DIFFERENTIAL FORMS
39.1. Background
Topics: diÔ¨Äerential forms, wedge product.
Although diÔ¨Äerential forms can be deÔ¨Āned in spaces of any dimension, in this course we will
work only with forms deÔ¨Āned on subsets of R3. In the following take U to be a subset of R3.
39.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. A 0-form on U is a smooth scalar Ô¨Āeld deÔ¨Āned on U.
Example: ŌČ = x2y + sin(xz) is a 0-form on R3.
39.1.2. DeÔ¨Ānition. A 1-form on U is an expression of the form
ŌČ = P(x, y, z) dx + Q(x, y, z) dy + R(x, y, z) dz
(1)
where P, Q, and R are smooth scalar Ô¨Āelds (0-forms) deÔ¨Āned on U. We usually write just
ŌČ = P dx + Q dy + R dz.
Example: ŌČ = x2y dx + xyz dy + eyz3 dz is a 1-form on R3.
Example: ŌČ = x sin x dx + y ln x dy is a 1-form on the right half plane.
Example: ŌČ = y2 arctan y dy is a 1-form on the real line.
39.1.3. DeÔ¨Ānition. A 2-form on U is an expression of the form
ŌČ = P(x, y, z) dy ‚ąßdz + Q(x, y, z) dz ‚ąßdx + R(x, y, z) dx ‚ąßdy
(2)
where P, Q, and R are smooth scalar Ô¨Āelds (0-forms) deÔ¨Āned on U. We usually write
ŌČ = P dy ‚ąßdz + Q dz ‚ąßdx + R dx ‚ąßdy.
Example: ŌČ = xyz dy ‚ąßdz + yz dz ‚ąßdx + z dx ‚ąßdy is a 2-form on R3.
Example: ŌČ =
xy
x2 + y2 dx ‚ąßdy is a 2-form on R2 with the origin excluded.
39.1.4. DeÔ¨Ānition. A 3-form on U is an expression of the form
ŌČ = P(x, y, z) dx ‚ąßdy ‚ąßdz
(3)
where P is a smooth scalar Ô¨Āeld (0-form) deÔ¨Āned on U. We usually write
ŌČ = P dx ‚ąßdy ‚ąßdz.
Example: ŌČ = (x2 + y2 + z2) dx ‚ąßdy ‚ąßdz is a 3-form on R3.
The n-forms above (for n = 0, 1, 2, 3) are examples of differential forms. It will be conve-
nient (for the purposes of this course) to regard a p-form for p > 3 as identically 0. We denote by
Gp(U) the family of p-forms on the region U. Thus (in this course), G4(U) = G5(U) = ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ = 0.
Terms of diÔ¨Äerential forms which are multiplied by 0 are omitted. For example, the 1-form
P dx is understood to be P dx + 0 dy + 0 dz and the 2-form Q dz ‚ąßdx is understood to be 0 dy ‚ąß
dz + Q dz ‚ąßdx + 0 dx ‚ąßdy.
Let P, Q, and R be scalar Ô¨Āelds on U. We say that ŌČ = P dx + Q dy + R dz is the 1-form
associated with the vector Ô¨Āeld F = P i + Q j + R k. (And, of course, F is the vector Ô¨Āeld
307


308
39. DIFFERENTIAL FORMS
associated with ŌČ.) Notice that a vector Ô¨Āeld is identically zero if and only if its associated 1-form
is.
Example: If F(x, y, z) = x2y i + y sin z j + exz k, then its associated 1-form is x2y dx + y sin z dy +
exz dz.
DiÔ¨Äerential forms can be added. Addition of 0-forms (scalar Ô¨Āelds) is familiar. We add 1-forms
by adding ‚ÄúcoeÔ¨Écients‚ÄĚ. If ŌČ = P dx + Q dy + R dz and ¬Ķ = S dx + T dy + U dz, then
ŌČ + ¬Ķ = (P + S) dx + (Q + T) dy + (R + U) dz.
Addition of 2-forms and 3-forms is similar.
DiÔ¨Äerential forms can be multiplied. The (wedge) product of a p-form ŌČ and a q-form ¬Ķ is
a (p + q)-form denoted by ŌČ ‚ąß¬Ķ. The wedge product has the following properties:
(1) The operation ‚ąßis associative. That is, if ŌČ, ¬Ķ, and őĹ are diÔ¨Äerential forms, then
(ŌČ ‚ąß¬Ķ) ‚ąßőĹ = ŌČ ‚ąß(¬Ķ ‚ąßőĹ);
(2) The distributive laws hold: if ŌČ ‚ąąGp and ¬Ķ,őĹ ‚ąąGq, then
ŌČ ‚ąß(¬Ķ + őĹ) = ŌČ ‚ąß¬Ķ + ŌČ ‚ąßőĹ
and
(¬Ķ + őĹ) ‚ąßŌČ = ¬Ķ ‚ąßŌČ + őĹ ‚ąßŌČ;
(3) If ŌČ is a 1-form, then ŌČ ‚ąßŌČ = 0.
Note: The wedge symbol is usually not written when taking the product of a 0-form with another
diÔ¨Äerential form. For example, if we multiply the 0-form 3x2z by the 2-form dx‚ąßdy we would write
3x2z dx ‚ąßdy instead of 3x2z ‚ąßdx ‚ąßdy. Furthermore, 0-forms are usually written on the left. Thus,
for example, if ŌČ = 3x dx and ¬Ķ = 2xz dy, then ŌČ ‚ąß¬Ķ = 6x2z dx ‚ąßdy. Note also that the number 0
acts as an annihilator on diÔ¨Äerential forms; that is, if ŌČ is a k-form, then 0 ¬∑ ŌČ is the zero k-form.
39.1.5. Theorem. If ŌČ and ¬Ķ are 1-forms, then
ŌČ ‚ąß¬Ķ = ‚ąí¬Ķ ‚ąßŌČ.
Proof. You are asked to prove this fact as problem 1 below.
39.1.6. Example. If ŌČ = x dx + y dy and ¬Ķ = y dx + x dy, then
ŌČ ‚ąß¬Ķ = (x dx + y dy) ‚ąß(y dx + x dy)
= xy dx ‚ąßdx + x2 dx ‚ąßdy + y2 dy ‚ąßdx + xy dy ‚ąßdy
[by (2) above]
= x2 dx ‚ąßdy + y2 dy ‚ąßdx
[by (3) above]
= (x2 ‚ąíy2) dx ‚ąßdy
[ by the preceding theorem]
39.1.7. Example. If ŌČ = 2xy dx + x2 dy and ¬Ķ = x dx + y dy + z dz, then
ŌČ ‚ąß¬Ķ = (2xy dx + x2 dy) ‚ąß(x dx + y dy + z dz)
= 2x2y dx ‚ąßdx + 2xy2 dx ‚ąßdy + 2xyz dx ‚ąßdz + x3 dy ‚ąßdx + x2y dy ‚ąßdy + x2z dy ‚ąßdz
= (2xy2 ‚ąíx3) dx ‚ąßdy ‚ąí2xyz dz ‚ąßdx + x2z dy ‚ąßdz.

(1) Let ŌČ = (3x + yz) dx + cos(xz) dy + (x2 + y) dz and ¬Ķ = (x + x2) dx + ey dy + (y + z) dz.

dx +

THE EXTERIOR DIFFERENTIAL OPERATOR
40.1. Background
Topics: exterior diÔ¨Äerential operator.
40.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. The exterior differentiation operator d is a mapping which takes
k-forms to (k + 1)-forms. That is d: Gk
/ Gk+1. It has the following properties:
(i) If f is a 0-form on R3, then d(f) is just the diÔ¨Äerential (or total derivative) df of f. That
is,
d(f) = df = fx dx + fy dy + fz dz;
(ii) d is linear (that is, if ŌČ and ¬Ķ are k-forms and c is a constant, then d(ŌČ + ¬Ķ) = dŌČ + d¬Ķ
and d(cŌČ) = c dŌČ);
(iii) d2 = 0 (that is, d(dŌČ) = 0 for every k-form ŌČ);
(iv) If ŌČ is a k-form and ¬Ķ is any diÔ¨Äerential form
d(ŌČ ‚ąß¬Ķ) = (dŌČ) ‚ąß¬Ķ + (‚ąí1)kŌČ ‚ąßd¬Ķ.
Example: If ŌČ = xy2 sin z, then
dŌČ = y2 sin z dx + 2xy sin z dy + xy2 cos z dz
[by (i)]
Example: If ŌČ = x2ez, then
dŌČ = 2xez dx + x2ez dz
[by (i)]
Example: If ŌČ = xy2z3 dy, then
dŌČ = d(xy2z3) ‚ąßdy + xy2z3 d(dy)
[by (iv)]
= d(xy2z3) ‚ąßdy
[by (iii)]
= (y2z3 dx + 2xyz3 dy + 3xy2z2 dz) ‚ąßdy
[by (i)]
= y2z3 dx ‚ąßdy + 2xyz3 dy ‚ąßdy + 3xy2z2 dz ‚ąßdy
= y2z3 dx ‚ąßdy ‚ąí3xy2z2 dy ‚ąßdz
Example: If ŌČ = xy dx + x2yz dy + z3 dz, then
dŌČ = d(xy dx) + d(x2yz dy) + d(z3 dz)
[by (ii)]
= d(xy) ‚ąßdx + xy d(dx) + d(x2yz) ‚ąßdy + x2yz d(dy)
+ d(z3) ‚ąßdz + z3 d(dz)
[by (iv)]
= d(xy) ‚ąßdx + d(x2yz) ‚ąßdy + d(z3) ‚ąßdz
[by (iii)]
= (y dx + x dy) ‚ąßdx + (2xyz dx + x2z dy + x2y dz) ‚ąßdy
+ (3z2 dz) ‚ąßdz
[by (i)]
= x dy ‚ąßdx + 2xyz dx ‚ąßdy + x2y dz ‚ąßdy
= x(2yz ‚ąí1) dx ‚ąßdy ‚ąíx2y dy ‚ąßdz.

41.1. Background
Topics: Hodge star operator.
41.1.1. DeÔ¨Ānition. In R3 the Hodge star operator ‚ąómaps k-forms to (3 ‚ąík)-forms for
0 ‚Č§k ‚Č§3. That is, if ŌČ is a k-form then ‚ąóŌČ is a (3 ‚ąík)-form. The operator has the following
properties:
(i) ‚ąó(ŌČ + ¬Ķ) = ‚ąóŌČ + ‚ąó¬Ķ if ŌČ and ¬Ķ are k-forms;
(ii) ‚ąó(fŌČ) = f(‚ąóŌČ) if f is a 0-form and ŌČ is a k-form;
(iii) ‚ąó1 = dx ‚ąßdy ‚ąßdz;
(iv) ‚ąódx = dy ‚ąßdz; ‚ąódy = dz ‚ąßdx; ‚ąódz = dx ‚ąßdy; and
(v) ‚ąó‚ąóŌČ = ŌČ for every k-form ŌČ.
Note: In even dimensional spaces Rn (n an even integer) condition (v) must be replaced by
‚ąó‚ąóŌČ = (‚ąí1)k(n‚ąík)ŌČ when ŌČ is a k-form.
41.1.2. Example. Let ŌČ = x2yz dy ‚ąßdz + y sin z dx ‚ąßdy + exy dx ‚ąßdz. Then
‚ąóŌČ = x2yz dx ‚ąíexy dy + y sin z dz.
