CHAPTER 43
MANIFOLDS AND ORIENTATION
43.1. Background‚ÄĒThe Language of Manifolds
Topics: manifolds, parametrization, orientation, unit normal vector
In these notes, for simplicity, we consider only manifolds contained in R3. For more serious
applications manifolds in Euclidean spaces Rn of arbitrary dimensions must be considered.
A 0-manifold is a point (or Ô¨Ānite collection of points).
A function is smooth if it is inÔ¨Ānitely diÔ¨Äerentiable (that is, if it has derivatives of all orders).
A curve is a continuous image of a closed line segment in R.
Let C be a curve.
The choice of an interval [a, b] and a continuous function f such that
C = f([a, b]) is a parametrization of C. If the function f is smooth, we say that C is a smooth
curve.
A 1-manifold is a curve (or Ô¨Ānite collection of curves). A 1-manifold is flat if it is contained
in some line in R3. For example, the line segment connecting two points in R3 is a Ô¨āat 1-manifold.
A surface is a continuous image of a closed rectangular region in R2.
Let S be a surface. The choice of an interval R = [a1, b1] √ó [a2, b2] and a continuous function f
such that S = f(R) is a parametrization of S. If the function f is smooth, we say that S is a
smooth surface.
A 2-manifold is a surface (or Ô¨Ānite collection of surfaces).
A 2-manifold is flat if it is
contained in some plane in R3. For example, the triangular region connecting the points (1, 0, 0),
(0, 1, 0), and (0, 0, 1) is a Ô¨āat 2-manifold.
A solid is a continuous image of the 3-dimensional region determined by a closed rectangular
parallelepiped (to avoid a ten-syllable name many people say rectangular solid or even just box)
in R3.
Let E be a solid. The choice of a rectangular parallelepiped P = [a1, b1] √ó [a2, b2] √ó [a3, b3] and
a continuous function f such that E = f(P) is a parametrization of E. If the function f is
smooth, we say that E is a smooth solid.
A 3-manifold is a solid (or Ô¨Ānite collection of solids).
43.1.1. Notation. Let P, Q, and R be points in R3. We denote by ‚ü®P, Q‚ü©the oriented line segment
starting at P and ending at Q. (It should be clear from context when this notation indicates a
line segment and when it indicates an inner product.) Also we denote by ‚ü®P, Q, R‚ü©the oriented
triangular region whose vertices are P, Q, and R and whose orientation is P Ô¨Ārst, then Q, then R.
Notice that ‚ąā‚ü®P, Q‚ü©= Q ‚ąíP and that ‚ąā‚ü®P, Q, R‚ü©= ‚ü®P, Q‚ü©+ ‚ü®Q, R‚ü©+ ‚ü®R, P‚ü©.
329


330
43. MANIFOLDS AND ORIENTATION
In the following paragraphs we will lay out some conventions for assigning orientations to the
manifolds we are considering. It should be noted that not every manifold admits an orientation.
When, for example, there is no way of assigning in a continuous fashion a unit tangent vector to
each point of a smooth 2-manifold, as is the case for a M¬®obius strip, we say that the manifold is
nonorientable.
Each of the manifolds we consider can be given two orientations, which we call, somewhat
arbitrarily, positive and negative. If M is an oriented manifold (that is, a manifold together with
an orientation), we denote by ‚ąíM the same manifold with the opposite orientation. Furthermore,
if M1 and M2 are oriented k-manifolds, we denote by M1 +M2 the union of M1 and M2 where each
part keeps its original orientation.
Is there any point to deÔ¨Āning ‚ąíM and M1 + M2? For our purposes there is exactly one reason:
it has to do with integration. Shortly we will discuss (for k = 0, 1, 2, and 3) what it means to
integrate a diÔ¨Äerential form over an oriented manifold. We will adopt the following very important
conventions:
(1) Whenever M is an oriented k-manifold and ŌČ is a k-form deÔ¨Āned on some open set con-
taining M, we deÔ¨Āne
Z
‚ąíM
ŌČ := ‚ąí
Z
M
ŌČ .
(2) Whenever M1 and M2 are oriented k-manifolds and ŌČ is a k-form deÔ¨Āned on some open
set containing M1 ‚ą™M2, we deÔ¨Āne
Z
M1+M2
ŌČ :=
Z
M1
ŌČ +
Z
M2
ŌČ, .
Oriented points
A point P can be assigned one of two orientations: +1 or ‚ąí1. There is no geometrical signiÔ¨Ā-
cance in this assignment.
Oriented curves
An example of a Ô¨āat 1-manifold is an interval in R. Its positive orientation is taken to be in
the direction of increasing values. That is, if you think of the interval as a subset of the x-axis; its
positive orientation is from left to right.
The orientations of an orientable 1-manifold are induced by its parametrization. If
r(t) = (r1(t), r2(t), r3(t)) (where a ‚Č§t ‚Č§b) is the parametrization of a smooth curve C in R3, the
positive orientation of C is in the direction from its ‚Äústarting point‚ÄĚ r(a) to its ‚Äúending point‚ÄĚ r(b).
The orientation of a 1-manifold induces an orientation on its boundary. If r(t) =
(r1(t), r2(t), r3(t)) (a ‚Č§t ‚Č§b) is the parametrization of the curve C, then the boundary of C
consists of its positively oriented ending point together with its negatively oriented starting point;
that is,
‚ąāC = r(b) + (‚ąír(a)) .
43.1.2. CAUTION. If P and Q are points most people write Q ‚ąíP for Q + (‚ąíP). Do not
interpret this or the right side of the preceding formula in terms of the ordinary vector arithmetic
on coordinates of points in R3.
Oriented surfaces
An example of a Ô¨āat 2-manifold is a rectangle in R2. Let R be the rectangle [a1, b1] √ó [a2, b2]
in the xy-plane. Its positive orientation is the one determined by the unit vector k = i √ó j. This
is often described as the ‚Äúcounterclockwise‚ÄĚ orientation. Of course, the connection between these
two descriptions is given by the familiar ‚Äúright-hand rule‚ÄĚ.


ORIENTED SOLIDS

An orientation of a 2-manifold is induced by its parametrization. Suppose that
r(u, v) = (r1(u, v), r2(u, v), r3(u, v)) (where a1 ‚Č§u ‚Č§b1 and a2 ‚Č§v ‚Č§b2) is the parametriza-
tion of a surface S. Then we take the positive orientation of S to be in the direction of its normal
vector ru √ó rv.
An orientation of a 2-manifold induces an orientation on its boundary. Suppose that
r(u, v) = (r1(u, v), r2(u, v), r3(u, v)) (a1 ‚Č§u ‚Č§b1 and a2 ‚Č§v ‚Č§b2) is the parametrization of a
surface S and that S is bounded by a Ô¨Ānite collection of simple closed piecewise smooth curves C1,
. . . , Cp. We write
‚ąāS =
p
X
k=1
Ck
where we have chosen the orientation on each Ck by selecting a parametrization of Ck in such a
way that at each point p where the parametrization is diÔ¨Äerentiable the binormal vector B(p) =
T(p) √ó N(p) points away from the surface. (Here, T(p) is the unit tangent vector to the curve Ck
at p and N(p) is the usual unit normal which results from normalizing ru √ó rv.) A somewhat more
informal way of saying the same thing is, ‚Äúif you walk around each of the curves Ck in a positive
direction with your head pointing in the direction of N, then the surface will always be on your
left.‚ÄĚ
Oriented solids
An example of a Ô¨āat 3-manifold is a rectangular parallelepiped in R3. Let R = [a, b]√ó[c, d]√ó[e, f]
in R3. Just as we think of the positive (counterclockwise) orientation in R2 as ‚Äúgoing from the
x-axis to the y-axis‚ÄĚ we specify the positive orientation in R3 by selecting Ô¨Ārst the x-axis, then the
y-axis, and Ô¨Ānally the z-axis. Any cyclic permutation of this choice (Ô¨Ārst y then z then x‚ÄĒor‚ÄĒ
Ô¨Ārst z then x then y) is also regarded as positive. Other permutations (Ô¨Ārst y then x then z, for
example) produce negative orientations.
An orientation of a 3-manifold is induced by its parametrization. If r(u, v, w) =
(r1(u, v, w), r2(u, v, w), r3(u, v, w)) (where a1 ‚Č§u ‚Č§b1 and a2 ‚Č§v ‚Č§b2 and a3 ‚Č§w ‚Č§b3) is the
parametrization of a solid E, the orientation of E is positive if the Jacobian of r is everywhere
positive.
An orientation of a 3-manifold induces an orientation on its boundary. Suppose
that r(u, v, w) = (r1(u, v, w), r2(u, v, w), r3(u, v, w)) (where a1 ‚Č§u ‚Č§b1 and a2 ‚Č§v ‚Č§b2 and
a3 ‚Č§w ‚Č§b3) is a positive parametrization of a solid E and that E is bounded by a Ô¨Ānite collection
of simple closed piecewise smooth surfaces S1, . . . , Sp. We write
‚ąāE =
p
X
k=1
Sk
where we have chosen the orientation on each Sk by selecting a parametrization of Sk in such a
way that at each point p where the parametrization is diÔ¨Äerentiable the normal vector N(p) points
away from the solid. That is, the positive orientation induced by a solid on its boundary is the
‚Äúoutward directed normal‚ÄĚ.

46.1. Background
Topics: Stokes‚Äô theorem, divergence theorem, Green‚Äôs theorem, generalized Stokes‚Äô theorem.
Here is perhaps the most important theorem in calculus.
This is certainly the result that
deserves to be called the fundamental theorem of calculus.
46.1.1. Theorem (generalized Stokes‚Äô theorem). Let M be a bounded smooth oriented k-manifold
and ŌČ be a smooth (k ‚ąí1)-form on an open set containing M. If M has a nonempty piecewise
smooth boundary ‚ąāM given the induced orientation , then
Z
M
dŌČ =
Z
‚ąāM
ŌČ .
If ‚ąāM is empty, then
Z
M
dŌČ = 0 .

46.3. Problems
(1) Let M be a closed bounded interval in R and let f be a 0-form on M (that is, a smooth real
valued function on M). What classical theorem do we get when we apply the generalized
Stokes‚Äô theorem to the 1-manifold M and the diÔ¨Äerential 0-form f? Explain.
(2) Let M be a smoothly parametrized curve in R3 and let f be a 0-form on some open subset
U of R3 containing M (that is, a smooth real valued function on U).
What classical
theorem do we get when we apply the generalized Stokes‚Äô theorem to the 1-manifold M
and the diÔ¨Äerential 0-form f? Explain.
(3) Let R be a simple region in the plane R2 whose boundary is a positively oriented simple
closed curve, let F be a vector Ô¨Āeld on a region in R2 containing R, and let ŌČ be the 1-
form associated with F. What classical theorem do we get when we apply the generalized
Stokes‚Äô theorem to the 2-manifold R and the diÔ¨Äerential 1-form ŌČ? Explain.
(4) Let S be a smoothly parametrized surface in R3 whose boundary is a positively oriented
simple closed curve, let F be a vector Ô¨Āeld on a region in R3 containing S, and let ŌČ
be the 1-form associated with F. What classical theorem do we get when we apply the
generalized Stokes‚Äô theorem to the 2-manifold S and the diÔ¨Äerential 1-form ŌČ? Explain.
(5) Let E be a simple solid region whose boundary has positive (outward) orientation, let F
be a vector Ô¨Āeld on a region in R3 containing E, and let ŌČ be the 1-form associated with F.
What classical theorem do we get when we apply the generalized Stokes‚Äô theorem to the
3-manifold E and the diÔ¨Äerential 2-form ‚ąóŌČ? Explain.
(6) By direct computation verify Stokes‚Äô theorem for the case of the vector Ô¨Āeld F(x, y, z) =
xy i+yz j+x2 k and the surface S which is the boundary of the unit cube [0, 1]√ó[0, 1]√ó[0, 1]
with the face in the xy-plane missing.
(7) By direct computation verify Stokes‚Äô theorem for the case of the vector Ô¨Āeld F(x, y, z) =
z i + x j + y k and the helicoid S whose parametrization is r(u, v) = (u cos v, u sin v, v) for
